The Suktasankirtana is a historical Mah√°k√°vya describing the good deeds of Vastup√¢la. It consists of eleven cantos, and at the end of each canto are appended five verses composed by Amara Pandita. Three out of these are eulogistic, the fourth mentions Arisumba as the author of this work and praises his poetical skill, while the fifth records the fact that these four verses are composed by Amara.

** The popularity of the work. **The Sukrtasankirtana does not seem to have been p even among the Jains. Verses from Some≈õvara’s Kirtikaumudi are often found in the Prabhako sa and Jinaharsha’s Vastup√¢lacharitra, but none from the Suktasankirtana. Balachandra has also com posed his Vasant√°vilasa on the style of the Kirtikaumudi.

It is not clear to what caste he belonged, whether he was a Bania or a bard. He has got the appellation Thakkura and this appellation was

** Analysis of the Work** The first canto gives the genealogy of the Ch√¢potkata kings Of Vanaraja it is said that he founded the city of Anahilla Pattana and that he erected there the temple of P√¢nch√¢sara P√¢r≈õvan√¢tha.

This list of Ch√¢vd√¢ Lings is identical with that given in the Sakrtak√Ærtikallolin√Æ of Udayaprabha In view of the concurrence of these two authorities, the statement in Krishn√¢ji’s Ratnam√¢l√¢, which is not so old as is believed, is unreliable.

The second canto describes the reigns of the Chaulukya kings M√ªlr√¢ja’s pilgrimage to Somes’vara every Monday is also mentioned in B√¢lachandra’s Vasantavil√¢sa. He had the biruda of Jagajzampana This biruda is found in he Kum√¢rap√¢lapratibodha, Kirtikaumudi, Suk·πõta-

kirtikalloloni and Vasantavil√°sa. Durlabhar√°ja was very modest and was ashamed when his court poets compared him to Krishna. Bhima defeated Bhojs of Dh√¢r√¢. Karna conquered the king of M√¢lv√¢ and brought home from there an image of Nilakantha Siva. Jayasimha conquered Barbaraka and took prisoner Yas’ovarma, king of Dh√¢r√¢. He had the tank called Siddhasaras dug and a high pillar of victory built. Jayasimha was very devotional towards his mother. Kum√¢rap√¢la abolished the confiscation of the property of tradesmen dying without male heirs and caused Jaina temples to be built in every city. He conquered the king of J√¢ngla, Arnor√¢ja of S√¢kambhari, and his general √Çmba·∏ça, who was a Bania, defeated and killed the Kadamba king Mallika Arjuna of the Konkana. This victory of Amba·∏ça is coroborated by B√¢lchandra in his Vasantavil√¢sa. Ajayadeva generally called Ajayap√¢la got from the king of S√¢mbhara a golden Man·∏çapik√¢. M√ªlar√¢ja II, though a child, defeated the Turushkas, Mahamad Sahabuddin Ghori. Bhimadeva II was very charitable and extravagant. His kingdom was being devoured by his powerful Man·∏çalesas, whom he was unable to control. Bhima was thus filled with anxiety about the fate of his kingom. One

night a glorious and splendid god, the spirit of his grand-father Kum√¢rap√¢la, appeared to him in a dream and said that in order to restore order and to prevent dismemberment of the kingdom and to save the Jain faith, which was almost sinking, he should make Lava·πáapras√¢da, son of Ar≈Üor√¢ja, son of Dhavala, to whom he had given the principality of Bh√Æmapall√Æ, his Sarve≈õvara and make his son Viradhavala his Yuvar√¢ja. V√Æradhavala then requested king Bhima to give him good counsellors. Bhima said that there served in this realm, Chan·∏çapa fiery in splendour, of the Porv√•da lineage. His son was Chan·∏çapras√¢da. His son Soma, who served under Jayasimha, acknowledged no master but king Siddhar√¢ja Jayasimha, and no god but the lord of the Jinas. His son A≈õvar√¢ja who made the universe splendid with his glory made seven pilgrimages in order to escape seven hells. His wife Kum√¢radevi, though eminent in the Jaina religon, also had faith in Siva. They had three sons‚ÄìMalladeva, Vastup√¢la & Tejahp√¢la Bhima then gave Vastup√¢la and Tejahp√¢la as Viradhavala’s counsellors, and said to him that his heroism, which will have sight with these two as eyes, may now trample down his enemies by xxxxxxg them out. The two ministers should fulfil the message of Kum√¢rap√¢la-the glorification of

the Jaina faith This account, though it materially differs from that given by Somesvara in his K√Ærtikaumudi, agrees with that in the two other contemporary works‚Äì(1) Jayasimha’s Vastup√¢la-Tejahp√¢laprasasti & (2) Udayaprabha’s Sukrta k√Ærtikallolini Vastupala’s own words should have, however, more weight in this respect In the Naran√¢r√¢yan√¢nanda he calls himself as the high minister of the Gurjaresvara and in its last canto he says that he accepted the dependence of the highministership of Bh√Æma, the lord of Gujarat, for the incessant occurence of the festival of faith without any obstacle, which is sweet on account of itssplendid ‚Åµpower B√•lchandra’s account, however, agrees with that of Somesvara

In the fourth canto Arƒ±sƒ±mha says that Viradhavala with the help of his ministers conquered the earth and put down all wrong and violence Tejahp√¢la then requested Vastup√¢la to keep in mind the king’s command and support the Jaina religion. Shanti Suri and Amara Sari, who received from king Jayasimha the title-" Tiger Cubs " as they had overcome proud disputants even in their infancy (4) Haribhadra’Stri (5) Vijayasena S√ªri. Vijayasena Suri explained to them the religious merits of becoming a Sangh√•dhipati. Vastupala resolved to do so.

In the fifth canto are described the preparations for the great pilgimage. Narachandra S√ªri of the Maladh√¢ri gachchha and spiritual adviser of Vastupala on his mother’s side, Jinadatta S√ªri of the V√¢yada gachchha, Santi Sari of the Sanderaka gachchha and Vardham√¢na S√ªri of the Gallaka people were among the notable Acharyas who accompanied. Mention is made of a halt at Kasahrada, modern Kasandra, and of the institution of a great festival in the temple of Rshabha.

In the sixth canto we have a conventional description of the sun-rise.

In the seventh canto is described the ascent of the mountain and the devotional festivities. After paying his respects to Kaparadi Yaksha, the presiding deity of the Tirtha, Vastupala entered the main temple of Adinatha. The Sangha stayed on the mountain for eight days.

In the eighth the pilgrimage to Deva-

pattana and Mount Girnar is described. From ≈öantrunjaya the Sangha started to Devapattana for offering worship to Soman√¢tha. Thence it proceeded to Girnar. A camp was pitched at the foot of the mountain and a ‘festival was held. Then follows the description ’ of the worship of Neminatha and the festivals. Having paid homage to Amb√¢, the presiding deity of the Jain temples on Mt Girnar and Samba and Pradyumna who had obtained salvation here, the Sangha descended after a stay of eight days.

In the ninth is given a poetical description of the six seasons which the minister saw while descending.

In the tenth begins the return journey. The Sangha entered Vamanasthali -the modern Vanthali, in all magnificence. When the procession reached near Dholka, not only Tejahp√¢la but Viradhavala came out with citizens to receive him. For a fuller description of the pilgrimage compare the last canto of the Dharmabhyudaya and the cantos eleven and twelve of the Vasantavilasa.
